        function gptClean(gptText) {

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="30f7a7e10bfe4339b12cc424fef819f0">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'fb33f486-26f3-454c-8ae0-991335d34abb'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d113467153" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The development of high-performance sodium ion batteries (SIBs) is heavily relied on the exploration of the appropriate electrode material for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, which ought to feature merits of high capacity, easy-to-handle synthesis, high conductivity, expedite mass transportation, and stable structure upon charging–discharging cycle. Herein, a universal source-template method is reported to synthesize a variety of transition metal (e.g., V, Sb, W, Zn, Fe, Co, Ni, and Cu) selenides implanting on N doped 3D carbon nanoarchitecture hybrids (M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub>@3D-CN) with powerful Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonding rivet. Benefiting from the superior architecture and potent Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonding between Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and N-doped 3D carbon (3D-CN), the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN nanohybrids, as anode of SIBs, show high capacity, high-rate capability, and long-cycle durability, which can deliver a reversible capacity of as high as 386 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, retain 219&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and run durably over thousands of charging–discharging cycles. The Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN as anode is also evaluated by developing a full SIB by coupling with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, which can deliver high energy density and show excellent stability, shedding light on its potential in practical application.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202305503-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202305503-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Renewable energy has been undergoing rapid growth worldwide over the recent years, which thus call for the development of cost-effective and reliable electrochemical energy storage (EES) technology to implement their high-efficiency utilization. Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have pervaded our daily lives as one of the most successful commercial electrochemical technologies, thanks to its small yet powerful merits and long-life span. Nonetheless, the high cost and uneven distribution of rare lithium resources has aroused widespread concerns especially using for large-scale grid energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0001_R_d113466772e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), as a burgeoning substitute for LIBs, has recently attracted extensive interest due to similar working principle to LIBs and low cost of sodium sources. SIBs yet suffer from low capacityand dramatic volume expansion due to its relatively high mass and large sizeof Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) to Li<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0002_R_d113466772e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is desirable to explore high-performance materials for reversible Na<sup>+</sup> storage.</p>
         
         <p>Transition metal selenides (M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub>), by virtue of their high capacity based on multistep conversion reaction as well as superb rate performance, have been envisaged as the promising materials for electrochemical energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0003_R_d113466772e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Eng. J.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison to metal oxides and metal sulfides, transition metal selenides have relatively weaker bond strength of M-Se and smaller van der Waals forces with enlarged layer space. These characteristics are advantageous for storing more Na<sup>+</sup> ions, facilitating mass transportation, promoting reversible conversion reactions, and ultimately improving the capacity and rate capability of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0004_R_d113466772e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, metal selenides for Na<sup>+</sup> storage face challenges to meet the requirements for practical applications due to fast capacity decay induced by poor conductivity and large volume variation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0005_R_d113466772e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, design and fabrication of 3D carbon supporting metal selenides nanoarchitectures can improve the conductivity toward ions/electron transfer and afford the proper space for buffering the volume change.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0006_R_d113466772e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Matter">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, such nonpolar carbon matrix cannot fix and capture polar M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub> effectively on account of the limited positions of conjugated nonpolar carbon planes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0007_R_d113466772e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The resulting weak interfacial electric/chemical coupling between M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub> and carbon matrix still impede the charge-transfer and form voltage hysteresis during the cycling process.</p>
         
         <p>To achieve tight recombination of M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub> and carbon matrix, most researchers have focused on constructing chemical bonds to form M<span class="icomoon"></span>C or Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C to strengthen the coupling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0008_R_d113466772e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such strong coupling through chemical fixation between M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub> and carbon matrix could achieve following merits: 1) enabling a strong affinity of the carbon matrix to M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub> nanoparticles, which significantly ensures the structural integrity by preventing M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub> nanoparticles from aggregating and detaching even after a long-term cycling. 2) powerfully changing the structure of carbon matrix and serving as a stronger absorptive electronic unit to store Na<sup>+</sup>. 3) leading to an enhanced reaction kinetics, facilitating easier diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> between M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub> layers and promoting faster electron transfer from the carbon matrix to M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0008_R_d113466772e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0009_R_d113466772e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Electrochim. Acta">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, Copper selenide as one of the metal selenides with individual chemical/physical properties has been widely used in fields of energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0004_R_d113466772e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0008_R_d113466772e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se exhibits a unique characteristic in which the Cu ion resides in a superionic state, while the Se atom occupies a face-centered cubic position. This arrangement imparts Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se with an exceptional level of electrical conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0010_R_d113466772e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Technol.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the volume of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se space unit is much larger than other copper selenides with a similar crystal structure, which could accommodate more Na ions to achieve high capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0011_R_d113466772e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Ceram. Int.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the Cu deficiency can modify the crystal structures as well as electrical transport properties, facilitating the electron/ion transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0012_R_d113466772e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ChemSusChem">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the presence of Cu vacancies in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se not only provides additional active sites but also facilitates the reaction process by introducing extra phonon scattering, which enhances the overall efficiency of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0013_R_d113466772e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Acta Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the above discussion, constructing a Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se confined within carbon (Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/C) hybrids with powerful Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonding would be effective to enable high performance sodium batteries. However, there are still exiting significant limitations in methods of fabricating Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/C nanohybrids let alone Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/C with cogent interface coupling between Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and carbon matrix.</p>
         
         <p>We herein demonstrated a universal source-template route to implanting various metal selenides onto N doped porous carbon with formation of powerful riveted Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds within 3D nanoarchitecture hybrids (M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub>@3D-CN, M = V, Sb, W, Zn, Fe, Co, Ni, and Cu). Finally, the electrochemical properties of this novel riveted structural Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN were investigated as anode of SIBs and performed impressively by manifesting high reversible capacity, fast kinetics, and superb cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202305503-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202305503-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1a</a> schematically illustrates the source template approach to copper selenide nanoparticles implanting onto N doped 3D porous carbon nanohybrid (Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN). The synthesis can be implemented in two-step process: 1) the hydrothermal treatment of the mixtures consisting of chitosan, urea, copper nitrate and selenium dioxide in an enclosed autoclave, during which urea and chitosan will polymerize to carbonaceous polymer, accompanying with in situ reduction conversion of SeO<sub>2</sub> into Se microspheres implanting onto carbon (Se/C); 2) the subsequent pyrolysis of mixture of Cu precursors and Se/C in inert atmosphere (Ar) would induce the evaporation of Se spheres for selenizing reaction and carbonatization of the polymers with formation of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. It's worth pointing out that SeO<sub>2</sub> not only served as the template for forming the connective 3D macro-porous framework but also as source to synthesis metal selenide and form Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C riveted bonds. More importantly, the as-developed synthetic approach can be applied to prepare a variety of metal selenides implanting onto N doped 3D porous carbon hybrids (M<sub>m</sub>Se<sub>n</sub>@3D-CN, M = V, Sb, W, Zn, Fe, Co, and Ni), as verified by the corresponding field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images and X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S7</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305503-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/44ba94f5-56d9-42fb-8a67-e8a874280c4c/adfm202305503-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/44ba94f5-56d9-42fb-8a67-e8a874280c4c/adfm202305503-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f7f330d9-4b23-43b8-ade1-58fa446b3235/adfm202305503-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/44ba94f5-56d9-42fb-8a67-e8a874280c4c/adfm202305503-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305503-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305503" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration to synthesize Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN composite. b,c) SEM images, d,e) TEM images, f) HRTEM images, g) the corresponding HAADF-STEM image and EDS elemental mappings, h) XRD pattern, i) crystal structure and high resolution of j) Cu 2p, k) Se 3d spectra for the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1b,c</a> show the typical FESEM images of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, clearly confirming the formation of 3D porous nanoarchitecture with carbon nanosheets as the connected network. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images also manifest that Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se nanoparticles are implanted onto the connective 3D porous carbon framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1d,e</a>). Such hierarchical microporous conductive network could not only facilitate ion/electron transportation to ensure fast reaction kinetics, but also provide robust skeleton to enhance the structural stability during cycling. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1f</a>) indicates that the lattice spaces of nanoparticles are ≈0.333&nbsp;nm, which can be indexed to the (111) crystal plane of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se phase. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) show the high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) and Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) element mapping images of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, demonstrating homogeneous distribution of C, N, Cu, and Se. Contrastively, the SEM images of pure Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se nanoparticles and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se nanoparticles embedded within porous N-doped carbon (Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN) are displayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9,S10</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, the particle size of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se nanoparticles ranges from 60–100&nbsp;nm. The Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se nanoparticles and 3D-CN were mixed through a simple mechanical process to form Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN. The crystalline phases of the three samples were further proved by X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1h</a>, the diffraction peaks of all samples can be precisely attributed to cubic Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se (JCPDS No. 06–0680). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1i</a> shows the crystal structure model of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se, which is composed of CuSe<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron. The Raman spectrum of samples Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). The scattering peaks at 471 cm<sup>−1</sup> is supposed to be the stretch vibration of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0014_R_d113466772e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, there are two typical characteristic peaks at 1363 and 1575 cm<sup>−1</sup> that appeared in spectrum of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, can be indexed to the disordered amorphous carbon (D-band) and the crystalline graphitic carbon (G-band), respectively. And the peak intensity ratio of D peak and G peak (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) for Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN (1.13) is higher than Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN (1.05), indicating there are rich defective carbon in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN due to the Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0015_R_d113466772e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Carbon">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such abundant defects could provide more Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption active sites and boost surface electron transfer, thus pledging effective pseudocapacitive Na storage.</p>
         
         <p>The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was utilized to investigate surface chemical compositions and valences of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. As presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the survey XPS spectrum shows that Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN is composed of Cu, Se, C, N, and O elements. The high-resolution XPS spectrum of Cu 2p is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1j</a>. The peaks located at 932.7 and 952.6&nbsp;eV are related to Cu<sup>+</sup>, respectively. In addition, the peaks at 934.7 and 954.9&nbsp;eV can be assigned to the Cu<sup>2+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0016_R_d113466772e845" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ChemSusChem">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0001">1k</a> exhibits the high-resolution XPS spectrum of Se 3d, there are two peaks relating to Se<sup>2−</sup> with the binding energies of 53.7 and 54.5&nbsp;eV, while the peaks centered at 55.3 and 55.8&nbsp;eV can be corresponded to Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0009_R_d113466772e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Electrochim. Acta">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0017_R_d113466772e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-resolution C 1s spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information) displays the five peaks centered at 284.6 (CC), 285.8 (C<span class="icomoon"></span>N/C<span class="icomoon"></span>Se), 286.7 (C<span class="icomoon"></span>O), 287.8&nbsp;eV (O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO), and 288.7&nbsp;eV (Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C) respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0008_R_d113466772e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0018_R_d113466772e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both Se 3d and C 1s demonstrate the existence of Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, which could provide a strong coupling between carbon matrix and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and thus ensure a long cycle stability. For the high-resolution N 1s spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information), the curve can be deconvoluted into four peaks at ≈398.4 (pyridinic N), 399.5 (pyrrolic N), 401 (Graphitic N), and 403.7&nbsp;eV (Oxygenated N), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0019_R_d113466772e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high resolution XPS of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN is displayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14,S15</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, no Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds were found in Se 3d and C1s of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrates thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) curve of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN with a heating rate of 10&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> in air. The first mass loss occurs before 410&nbsp;°C, which can be ascribed to the evaporation of adsorbed water. The weight increase at around 410–500&nbsp;°C corresponds to the decomposition of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se to CuO and SeO<sub>2</sub>. The mass decrement starting at (500–600&nbsp;°C) region can be due to the removal of porous carbon and the sublimation of SeO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0020_R_d113466772e906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Technol. Adv. Sustainable Syst. Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the TGA results of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, the 3D-CN content in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN is calculated to be 21.6%. The nitrogen adsorption-desorption tests (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information) indicating that Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN consists of hierarchical porous structure with high surface area of 108.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. By contrast, the surface area of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se is 98.7 and 17.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The pore size distribution was further analyzed by the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method (inset of Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating that Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN composite possesses a hierarchical porous structure with micropores, mesopores, and macropores in hybrids. Such hierarchical porous structures could supply sufficient interfacial contact area toward active sites and the electrolyte with boosting both electrolyte and ions transport, thus increasing capacity and rate capability of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN.</p>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were primarily conducted to explore the significance of introducing normal carbon coating and carbon encapsulating with powerful Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0002">2</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the associated local Fermi softness [SF(r)] of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se, manifesting the higher reactivity of the active sites in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. Besides, the charge density difference aiming to comprehend the bonding properties of the adsorbed Na<sup>+</sup> is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0002">2b</a>. One can observe that Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN with powerful Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonding shows higher density of transferred charges, by contrast, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN show inferior ones. This phenomenon proves that the riveted Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C configuration could effectively increase the adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup>. In this respect, the 2D projection of the charge density contour for Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se are shown (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0002">2c</a>). With the red and blue indicating the higher and lower reactivity, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN significantly demonstrates more red areas, being in favor of more effective energy storage than the other two samples.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305503-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b9e5be21-54b0-404e-b45d-811ddb9a6874/adfm202305503-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b9e5be21-54b0-404e-b45d-811ddb9a6874/adfm202305503-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b7ecd4ca-f070-420a-9490-4e7b75f2c051/adfm202305503-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b9e5be21-54b0-404e-b45d-811ddb9a6874/adfm202305503-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305503-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305503" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculations. a) Top and side illustration of the local Fermi softness, the calculated charge density difference b) for Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se. c) 2D projection of charge density contour, d) Na ion adsorption energy at different active sites, e) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers, and f) Density of states (DOS) of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, the adsorption energy (ΔEa) of Na<sup>+</sup> on the active sites in different configurations (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18–S20</a>, Supporting Information) of three samples are calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0002">2d</a>), indicating the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption in Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C riveted Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. In order to learn more about the reaction kinetics of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se, the diffusion barrier energies and the minimum energy paths are investigated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). With the comparison of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se (0.18&nbsp;eV) and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN (0.30&nbsp;eV), the Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C riveted Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN (0.17&nbsp;eV) exhibits the lowest diffusion energy barrier, attesting its preferable diffusion kinetics. Moreover, the total density of states (TDOS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0002">2f</a>) and partial DOS (PDOS) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) are essentially studied. A higher DOS peak in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN near the Fermi level is obviously observed, indicating the facilitated electric conductivity. Additionally, the p orbital PDOS of the nitrogen atom in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN are further analyzed, demonstrating that the adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup> in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN was significantly strengthened owing to the powerful Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C chemical fixation. Overall, DFT results demonstrate the abundant active sites and enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption in Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C riveted Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, which could result in more rapid electrochemical reaction kinetics than the other two samples.</p>
         
         <p>Encouraged by the above DFT analysis, the half-cells were first assembled with Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN as anode and metallic sodium as counter electrode to evaluate their sodium storage properties. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the initial four cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode with a voltage window of 0.3–3&nbsp;V. The first cycle is inconsistent with subsequent three cycles, which may be associated with the formation of SEI layer and electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0021_R_d113466772e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the second cathodic scan, three reduction peaks located at 1.92, 1.63, and 0.67&nbsp;V are ascribed to the intercalation reactions of Na<sup>+</sup> into the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se lattice to form Na<sub>y</sub>Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and the conversion reaction of intermediate phases Na<sub>y</sub>Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se to Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Cu.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0022_R_d113466772e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent anodic sweep, two oxidation peaks at ≈1.52 and 2.04&nbsp;V, representing the reversible reaction from Cu and Na<sub>2</sub>Se to Na<sub>y</sub>Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and the desodiation process from Na<sub>y</sub>Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se to Cu<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0023_R_d113466772e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the storage mechanism of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN can be concluded as the following equation:</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305503-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca89d597-f1b7-4c0e-b036-0e85e3692aa7/adfm202305503-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>

         </section>

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The electrochemical behavior of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN exhibits similar redox reaction compared with Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a</a> (Supporting Information) displays the galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is consistent with the redox peaks in the CV profiles. The electrode delivers an initial discharge capacity of 386 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 95.6%. Such high ICE could be attributed to the splendid structural reversibility and the use of ether electrolyte. After 100 cycles, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN maintains a capacity of 339.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 88% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0003">3b</a>). By comparison, the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se delivers an obvious capacity decay within 30 cycles and the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN just presents a 66% retention after 100 cycles. Besides the significant stability, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN also exhibits fantastic rate capability. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0003">3c</a>, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode delivers reversible capacities of 366, 343, 325, 311, 297, 270, and 219 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. More impressively, when current density recovered to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the electrode still maintains the same reversible specific capacity as the initial cycles, indicating the excellent reversibility of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. In contrast, the reversible capacity of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN were not maintained well with the increased current density. In addition, the discharging–charging voltage curves at different current densities demonstrate further glorious rate properties of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24b</a>, Supporting Information). Such outstanding rate capability of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode outperforms previously reported copper selenide-based materials for sodium storage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0003">3d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0021_R_d113466772e1480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0022_R_d113466772e1480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0024_R_d113466772e1480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nanomaterials Chem. Commun. (Camb.)">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Inspired by the superior rate capability of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) depicts the kinetics analysis based on CV measurements at various scan rates from 0.2 to 1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The <i>b</i> values are calculated to be 0.867, 0.731, 0.813, 0.743, and 0.984 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25b</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that the charge storage is mainly dominated by pseudocapacitive behavior. Additionally, the capacitive-controlled contribution (92.4%) is procured for the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode at a scan rate of 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0003">3e</a>). As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0003">3f</a>, the capacitive contribution increases gradually with the increase of scan rates, indicating the capacitive charge storage accounts for a considerable proportion of the total capacity. For instance, of note, the capacitive contribution at each scan rate for Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN are higher than Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN, indicating the improved kinetics on account of the Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds formations and being consistent with DFT calculations (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). Thanks to the advantage of porous 3D structure and strong Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C riveted bonds, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode also displays superb long term cycling stability. The electrode delivers a stable average capacity of 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 1200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is superior to the cycle performance of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se electrode and previously Cu-based anode. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0003">3g</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). We have also tested the electrochemical performance of N doped macro-porous carbon (3D-CN), demonstrating inferior sodium storage properties (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27,S28</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the advantages of hierarchical porous structure and superb configuration, the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN still retain its porous morphology after 100 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information), manifesting the exceptional structure stability. Contrastively, the morphology of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se electrodes displays obvious variations after cycles (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30,S31</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32a</a> (Supporting Information) displays the Nyquist plots after 100 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> for Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se. According to the equivalent circuit model in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32c</a> (Supporting Information), the charge transfers resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode (71 Ω) is much lower than that of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN (158 Ω) and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se (263 Ω) electrode, implying improved charge transport process in the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. In addition, based on the Z’-<i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> (<i>ω</i> = 2<i>π</i>f) curves in the low-frequency region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32b</a>, Supporting Information), the calculated diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode is 2.64&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN (5.87&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−14</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), once more indicating the fast sodium ions diffusion kinetics of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode. Besides, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was conducted to well defined the D<sub>Na+</sub> during charging and discharging state. As exhibited in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33,S34</a> (Supporting Information), Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN also demonstrates a higher D<sub>Na+</sub> than Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se/3D-CN during both charge and discharge processes, further proving the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The faster Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN give the credit to the synergistic effects of configuration superiorities, riveted Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds and 3D hierarchical porous structure, dramatically accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup>/electron transport to realize rapid pseudocapacitive sodium storage.</p>
         
         <p>Due to rational structural design, Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN offers multiple advantages for Na-ion storage: 1) the Cu deficiency in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se can modify the crystal structures as well as electrical transport properties, vastly facilitating the electron/ion transfer. 2) the Cu defects not only supply more active sites, but also accelerate reaction process owing to the extra phonon scattering generated by vacancies, enabling high capacity and rapid kinetics. 3) The inter-crosslinked multi-porous carbon network in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN can promote the penetration of electrolyte and provide good electron transport paths. 4) The Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se nanoparticles are powerfully confined in porous carbon due to the riveted Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds, which not only inhibits the agglomeration of nanoparticles but also maintains the structural integrity. Benefiting from the above superiorities, the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN composite exhibits good cycling performance, excellent rate capability, and high capacity for sodium storage.</p>
         
         <p>In order to well understand the in-depth electrochemical reaction mechanism of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, other characterizations including ex situ XPS, XRD, and TEM were performed. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4a–c</a> displays the ex situ XPS spectrum of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrodes at various charge/discharge potentials (origin state, discharge to 0.3&nbsp;V/D-0.3&nbsp;V, charge to 3&nbsp;V/C-3&nbsp;V). For high resolution of Cu 2p at origin state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4a</a>), the Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> respectively presents two peaks at 932.7, 934.6 and 952.7, 955&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Cu<sup>+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup>. While at discharged state of 0.3&nbsp;V, the ratio of Cu<sup>+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> is significantly decreased, simultaneously the peak belonging to Cu<sup>0</sup> occurs, attributing to the formation of Cu metal and Na<sub>2</sub>Se from the multistep conversion reactions of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0004_R_d113466772e1683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the process of fully charged, the ratio of Cu<sup>0</sup> is correspondingly decreased, and the content of Cu<sup>2+</sup> is partly recovered, verifying the well reversibility of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0025_R_d113466772e1695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the high resolution of Se 3d spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4b</a>) also varies significantly as the charging/discharging processes go on. Obviously, when the electrode is discharged to 0.3&nbsp;V, the peaks of Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub> decrease to almost vanish, which indicates the strong interaction and conversion reactions between Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and Na. Notably, no sodium<span class="icomoon"></span>selenium bonds were detected here possibly on account of the oxidation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0026_R_d113466772e1712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While during fully charged state, these peaks of Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub> show an obvious recovery, which demonstrates the stable structure of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. The high resolution of C 1s (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4c</a>) shows that the peaks at the high binding energy of 286–291&nbsp;eV increase visibly during the discharging process and then decrease correspondingly but without vanishing ultimately after the fully charged state, which can be attribute to the SEI formation and sodium-containing substrates generations including Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, RCONa, R(O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO) Na, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0027_R_d113466772e1732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305503-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6c719d16-1a03-443e-8fa1-30602aa6272f/adfm202305503-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6c719d16-1a03-443e-8fa1-30602aa6272f/adfm202305503-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/34327a19-cc51-4082-b599-1b93ec970d49/adfm202305503-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6c719d16-1a03-443e-8fa1-30602aa6272f/adfm202305503-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305503-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305503" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ XPS spectra of a) Cu 2p, b) Se 3d, and c) C 1s at different electrochemical states during the first discharge/charge cycle. d) Charging/discharging curve of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. e) Ex-XRD pattern, f) Ex-HRTEM, g) HAADF-STEM image, and h) EDS elemental mappings of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode during the first discharge/charge cycle.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Ex situ XRD and HRTEM were conducted to systematically investigate the structural transition and phase evolution of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode upon Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4d</a> shows the discharge–charge curves, exhibiting different terminated electrochemical states to test phase transformation during the first cycle process. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4e</a>, at the original state, the reflections at 26.8<sup>o</sup> and 44.5° can be indexed to the phase Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se (JCPDS No. 06–0680), the strong peak at 43.4<sup>o</sup> is from Cu current collector. As the discharge process proceeds, the intensities of the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se peaks dramatically decrease, and correspondingly the weak peak of NaCuSe phase (JCPDS No. 71–1820) are presented at 32.5<sup>o</sup>, and the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>Se phase (JCPDS No. 77–2155) emerges at 37.8<sup>o</sup>, observably indicating the multistep conversion reactions between Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se and Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0022_R_d113466772e1792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While during the following charging process, NaCuSe and Na<sub>2</sub>Se phase fades gradually, the diffraction peak of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se accordingly increases, further indicating the reversible transformation process of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0004_R_d113466772e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The whole phase evolution processes are consistent with the CV results and conform to our prediction of the multistep conversion reactions with the intermediate phases generations. Besides, further evidence is given by the ex situ HRTEM, corresponding selected-area electron diffraction, HAADF-STEM, and EDS elemental mappings. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4f</a> depicts the HRTEM image as the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN is discharged to 0.3&nbsp;V, in which Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se, Na<sub>2</sub>Se, NaCuSe, Cu phase are all detected, manifesting the good consistency with above ex-situ XRD results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0022_R_d113466772e1819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202305503-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305503-bib-0028_R_d113466772e1819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a> (Supporting Information) shows the corresponding selected-area electron diffraction patterns, demonstrating consistent result as Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4f</a>. Furthermore, the HAADF-STEM, and EDS elemental mappings in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4g</a> demonstrate the well-maintained porous architecture and the homogeneous existence of C, N, Se, Cu, and Na elements in Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN. For the fully charged state, the HRTEM in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0004">4h</a> verifies the lattice distance of 0.33&nbsp;nm which can be corresponded to the (111) plane of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se, proving the high reversibility toward electrochemical reactions and structure integrity of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode.</p>
         
         <p>To further assess the application potential of the as-prepared Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN, we assembled sodium-ion full cells by employing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) electrode as cathode and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN electrode as anode (Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN//NVP SIBs), as displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0005">5a</a>. The morphology, XRD pattern and sodium storage performances of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a> (Supporting Information). In order to achieve the best overall performance, an optimized voltage window was fixed to be 1.0–3.7&nbsp;V and ideal mass ratio between the anode and cathode was controlled at ≈1: 4. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0005">5b</a> displays the charge–discharge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN anode. And Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0005">5c</a> shows the charge/discharge curves of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN//NVP SIBs full cells, which delivers the first charge/discharge capacity of 325/310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of anode), accompanied by an initial Coulombic efficiency of 95.4%. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0005">5d</a> presents the rate performance of Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN//NVP SIBs, with average discharge capacities of 318, 288, 260, 232, 208, and 170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Meanwhile, the SIBs full cells deliver a stable capacity output with a capacity retention of 80.9% after 600th cycles, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305503-fig-0005">5e</a>, outperforming many previously Cu-based sodium full cells (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, a respectable energy density of 203&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> can be obtained at a power density of 210&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>, which surpasses the other type of energy storage devices (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that the Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN has promising potential for energy storage applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305503-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/34259e27-be89-4d94-8cab-234aee8a7ebe/adfm202305503-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/34259e27-be89-4d94-8cab-234aee8a7ebe/adfm202305503-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2a53ed2d-a40d-4682-be66-68546a493d6b/adfm202305503-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/34259e27-be89-4d94-8cab-234aee8a7ebe/adfm202305503-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305503-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305503" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the SIBs full cells. b) Charge–discharge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN anode. c) Galvanostatic charge–discharge voltage profiles of full cell at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Rate capability and e) cycling performance of the full cells (Inset: LEDs powered by our full cells).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202305503-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202305503-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a facile and universal synthetic strategy has been developed to fabricate a variety of transition metal selenides nanoparticles implanted onto 3D hierarchical porous carbon interconnected architecture with powerful riveted Se<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds. Both half-cell and full-cell tests demonstrate Cu<sub>2−x</sub>Se@3D-CN is one of the promising anode materials for sodium ion batteries with high capacity, high-rate capability, and long-term cycling durability. Our design philosophy of electrode materials can be highly valuable for building up various metal selenides nanomaterials with promising applications in energy storage and other fields and beyond.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                        <span><a
